SCENARIO 167 — Formula functions: group_has, group_size, group_add, group_remove
---------------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mgrp7 GroupFormulas 8 8
!match use mgrp7
!ent add a A 30 1 1 10
!ent add b B 30 2 1 11
!ent group new swarm
!ent group add swarm a
!eval "group_has(\"swarm\", \"a\")"
!eval "group_has(\"swarm\", \"b\")"
!eval "group_size(\"swarm\")"
!eval "group_size(\"ghosts\")"
!eval "group_add(\"swarm\", \"b\")"
!eval "group_add(\"swarm\", \"b\")"
!eval "group_size(\"swarm\")"
!eval "group_remove(\"swarm\", \"a\")"
!eval "group_remove(\"swarm\", \"a\")"
!eval "group_remove(\"never_existed\", \"a\")"

Expected: group_has returns True for a (member), False for b. Initial
size is 1 for swarm, 0 for the non-existent 'ghosts' group (size of
an undefined group is 0, not an error — symmetric with the no-op
behavior of group_remove on a missing group). group_add returns
True the first time (newly added) and False on the duplicate. After
adding b, size is 2. group_remove returns True the first time and
False the second (a is no longer a member). Removing from a never-
existed group also returns False without raising.


SCENARIO 168 — self_id/current_id and group-gated passive
----------------------------------------------------------

!match new mgrp8 GroupPassive 8 8
!match use mgrp8
!ent add a A 30 1 1 10
!ent add b B 30 2 1 11
!ent group new sworn
!ent group add sworn b
!ent set_var b sworn_tick 0
!passive add b watch on_turn_start "entity[self].sworn_tick = entity[self].sworn_tick + (1 if group_has(\"sworn\", self_id()) else 0)"
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!eval "entity[b].sworn_tick"
!eval "group_remove(\"sworn\", \"b\")"
!turn next
!turn next
!eval "entity[b].sworn_tick"
!eval "current_id()"

Expected: B is in the 'sworn' group; the passive ticks on every
B-turn. Turn order is [b, a] (B's init=11 > A's init=10) but the
active index sticks to the first-spawned entity, so the cycle here
runs A → B → A, with B ticking once and A's turns not firing the
passive at all. sworn_tick is 1 after the three turn-nexts. After
group_remove, the passive still fires on B's turn but group_has
returns False, so the ternary contributes 0 — sworn_tick stays at 1.
current_id() returns whichever entity's turn it is at the time of
the eval; in the final call it's A.


SCENARIO 169 — Autosaves, manual save, and silent command undo
---------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mhist1 HistBasic 8 8
!match use mhist1
!ent add a A 30 1 1 10
!ent add b B 30 2 1 11
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!history list
!ent hp a -7
!history save before_more_damage
!ent hp a -7
!ent info a
!history list commands
!history undo command
!ent info a

Expected: Three turn-nexts open round 1 (A's turn) and advance into
round 2 (B then A) thanks to the spawn-order quirk that puts A at
active_index=1 initially. By that point the history holds 2 round
snapshots and 3 turn snapshots. !ent hp -7 creates a command
snapshot of the pre-damage state, then the manual save bookmarks
the post-7-damage state. A second -7 brings A to 16 HP. !ent info
is read-only — no extra command snapshot appears. !history list
commands shows just the two -7 commands. `undo command` is below
every confirmation threshold (default command threshold is -1 =
never prompts), so it executes silently, reverting A back to 23 HP.


SCENARIO 170 — Confirmation thresholds for destructive undos
-------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mhist2 HistConfirm 8 8
!match use mhist2
!ent add a A 30 1 1 10
!ent add b B 30 2 1 11
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!history undo turn
!history undo turn 2
!history undo turn 3
!history undo turn 3 confirm
!history undo round
!history undo round 1 confirm
!history undo to round 1
!history undo to round 1 confirm
!history undo command 99

Expected: Six turn-nexts cycle through several rounds. `undo turn`
and `undo turn 2` are below the default turn confirmation threshold
(3) and execute silently. `undo turn 3` hits the threshold and
returns the warning message instead of doing the undo — only when
the user re-issues with `confirm` does the undo actually run.
Likewise for `undo round` (default round threshold is 1, so even a
1-round undo prompts). `undo to round 1` always confirms because
it's a non-linear jump. The final `undo command 99` fails cleanly
because not that many command snapshots exist — the error message
names the actual retention limit so the user knows what to adjust.


SCENARIO 171 — Retention pruning of turn autosaves
---------------------------------------------------

!match new mhist3 HistRetention 8 8
!match use mhist3
!ent add a A 30 1 1 10
!ent add b B 30 2 1 11
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!history list
!system new tight
!system set tight autosave_turn_retention_rounds 1
!match new mhist4 HistTight 8 8 system=tight
!match use mhist4
!ent add a A 30 1 1 10
!ent add b B 30 2 1 11
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next

Expected: The first match uses default rules (turn retention = 3
rounds). After 8 turn-nexts it's deep into round 5; the turn
snapshot list spans only the most recent 3 rounds (rounds 3-5, so
roughly 5 entries depending on how many turns each round held).
Round snapshots are unlimited so all 5 are still there. The second
match uses a custom system with autosave_turn_retention_rounds=1;
after 4 turn-nexts only the current round's turn snapshots survive,
demonstrating that the rule is per-system and per-match.


SCENARIO 172 — Snapshot export and import as a manual save
-----------------------------------------------------------

!match new mhist5 HistExport 8 8
!match use mhist5
!ent add a A 30 1 1 10
!turn next
!history save checkpoint
!ent hp a -10
!history export manual:checkpoint /tmp/_hist_export_test.json
!history delete checkpoint
!history list manual
!history import /tmp/_hist_export_test.json as restored
!history list manual
!history restore restored confirm
!ent info a

Expected: `checkpoint` bookmarks A at 30 HP. After damaging A to 20,
the snapshot is exported to JSON, then the manual entry is deleted
(empty manual list afterward). Importing the JSON adds a manual save
named `restored` — the imported snapshot is always categorized as
manual regardless of its original kind, so it shows up in the
manual list. Restoring it (with confirm) puts A back to 30 HP. The
JSON file is a self-contained snapshot, usable as off-bot persistent
storage across bot restarts.


SCENARIO 173 — !store save with include_history round-trips autosaves

!ent add b B 30 2 1 11

!turn next

!store save /tmp/_hist_store_no.json
!store save /tmp/_hist_store_yes.json include_history=yes

Expected: !store save without the flag produces a smaller file and,
on reload, gives a match with empty history (no rounds, turns, or
manual saves — exactly the autosave-free state the user opted into
by NOT setting include_history). The second save with
`include_history=yes` round-trips the full autosave history; after

and the named manual save `important` is present.


SCENARIO 174 — random_int via eval and inside a passive
--------------------------------------------------------

!match new mrand1 RandInt 8 8
!match use mrand1
!ent add hero Hero 100 1 1 10
!eval random_int(1, 6)
!eval random_int(1, 6)
!eval random_int(-5, 5)
!ent set_var hero last_roll 0
!passive add hero burning on_turn_start "entity[self].last_roll = random_int(2, 8); entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - entity[self].last_roll"
!turn next
!ent info hero
!turn next
!ent info hero
!turn next
!ent info hero
!eval random_int(5, 3)
!eval "random_int('not', 'int')"
!eval random_string()

Expected: The first three !eval calls return integers in their
respective inclusive ranges — 1-6, 1-6, and -5 to 5. The values
themselves are non-deterministic (no shared seed across sessions)
but each call is guaranteed to be within the requested range.

The `burning` passive stores the rolled damage on the entity's
`last_roll` var, then subtracts that amount from HP. (Augmented
assignment like `hp += -x` isn't allowed by the formula engine —
write it out as `hp = hp - x`.) After each turn start, hero.hp has
dropped by whatever last_roll says — `!ent info hero` shows the
last_roll value alongside the lowered HP, and the two values agree
(HP decreases by exactly last_roll). Storing the roll on the
entity rather than re-rolling inside the if/else matters:
random_int returns a different value on each call, so "roll once,
branch on the stored value" is the standard idiom for formula-level
randomness.

The final three !eval calls demonstrate the validation errors:
random_int with lo > hi, random_int with non-integer args, and
random_string with no choices all surface as ❌ FormulaError with
messages that name the offending argument and its type — easier to
debug than the raw Python ValueError / IndexError underneath.


SCENARIO 175 — random_string with if/elif/else branching
---------------------------------------------------------

!match new mrand2 RandStr 8 8
!match use mrand2
!ent add hero Hero 100 1 1 10
!ent set_var hero status none
!ent set_var hero buff 0
!passive add hero mood on_turn_start "entity[self].status = random_string('rage', 'calm', 'fear'); entity[self].hp = max(0, entity[self].hp - 8) if entity[self].status == 'rage' else (min(entity[self].max_hp, entity[self].hp + 4) if entity[self].status == 'calm' else entity[self].hp); entity[self].buff = 0 if entity[self].status == 'rage' else (5 if entity[self].status == 'calm' else -2)"
!turn next
!ent info hero
!turn next
!ent info hero
!turn next
!ent info hero
!turn next
!ent info hero

Expected: The `mood` passive rolls a status on each turn-start —
one of 'rage', 'calm', 'fear' — stores it on the entity, then
branches:
  - rage   →  HP -8, buff cleared to 0
  - calm   →  HP +4 (clamped to max_hp), buff = 5
  - fear   →  buff = -2 (HP unchanged; the entity is too scared
              to swing but takes no damage either)

The formula above uses chained ternaries to fit on one line — when
typed into Discord directly the same logic reads more naturally as
if/elif/else:

    entity[self].status = random_string("rage", "calm", "fear")
    if entity[self].status == "rage":
        entity[self].hp = max(0, entity[self].hp - 8)
        entity[self].buff = 0
    elif entity[self].status == "calm":
        entity[self].hp = min(entity[self].max_hp, entity[self].hp + 4)
        entity[self].buff = 5
    else:
        entity[self].buff = -2

Both forms have identical semantics. Across four turns, the hero's
`status` and `buff` vars track whichever branch fired most recently
and HP drifts down or up depending on which moods came up. Because
random_string is non-deterministic the exact sequence varies, but
every turn the status is one of the three listed values and the
HP/buff pair is consistent with the branch that name implies — a
useful pattern for "random table" status effects.

The roll is assigned to entity[self].status FIRST, then the
ternary chain (or the if/elif) reads it back. Calling random_string
twice in a row would give two independent rolls, which is rarely
what a GM wants for a "roll once and react" pattern — using an
entity var (or any formula-readable storage) is the idiom for
memoizing the roll.


SCENARIO 176 — Team is now an entity var, readable and writable from formulas
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mteam1 TeamAsVar 8 8
!match use mteam1
!ent add a A 10 1 1 10
!ent add b B 10 2 1 11
!ent set_var a team red
!ent set_var b team blue
!ent info a
!ent info b
!eval entity[a].team
!eval entity[b].team
!eval 'entity[a].team == "red"'
!eval 'entity[b].team == "red"'
!eval 'entity[a].team = "purple"'
!ent info a
!eval entity[a].team
!system new sci_fi
!system set sci_fi team_var faction
!match new mteam2 SciFi 8 8 system=sci_fi
!match use mteam2
!ent add s1 Ship1 10 1 1 10
!ent set_var s1 faction federation
!ent info s1
!eval entity[s1].faction
!eval entity[s1].team

Expected: Team has been demoted from a dedicated dataclass field to
an ordinary entity var, configured via the new `team_var` rule
(default key: "team"). That means:

  - !ent set_var <id> team <value> is the canonical way to assign
    or change an entity's team. No special command is needed; teams
    are just strings stored under the team_var key.
  - !ent info still shows `Team: red` because the e.team property
    reads vars[team_var] under the hood, and the entity_line_format /
    entity_info_format templates haven't changed.
  - Formulas can read teams via entity[X].team (when team_var is
    "team"). String literals inside the formula need quotes that
    survive shell tokenization — wrap the whole expression in
    single quotes so the inner double quotes reach the formula
    engine: !eval 'entity[a].team == "red"' returns True, the
    mismatched variant returns False. Useful for "if entity[self]
    .team == entity[this].team" friendly-fire checks inside
    passives.
  - Formulas can WRITE teams the same way: entity[a].team = "purple"
    propagates through write_var (firing on_var_changed hooks, the
    same as any other var write) and !ent info reflects the new
    value on the next call.
  - The second match opts into a custom team_var = "faction" via a
    custom game system. The same entity now exposes its team as
    entity[s1].faction — entity[s1].team raises "Variable 'team' is
    not defined" because that key doesn't exist on the entity. The
    e.team Python property still works (it dereferences team_var per
    match), so the !ent info card still shows the team — only the
    formula path is sensitive to the renamed key, mirroring how
    hp_var/max_hp_var rename hp / max_hp in formulas without
    breaking !ent info's display.


SCENARIO 177 — Friendly-fire damage aura via a team check inside a global passive
----------------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mteam3 FriendlyFire 8 8
!match use mteam3
!ent add knight Knight 30 1 1 10
!ent add archer Archer 30 2 1 11
!ent add goblin Goblin 30 3 1 12
!ent set_var knight team alliance
!ent set_var archer team alliance
!ent set_var goblin team horde
!gpassive add aura on_round_start 'entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - 5 if entity[self].team != entity[this].team else entity[self].hp'
!turn next
!ent info knight
!ent info archer
!ent info goblin
!turn next
!turn next
!turn next
!ent info knight
!ent info archer
!ent info goblin

Expected: The `aura` global passive runs on_round_start, which fires
for EVERY entity in turn-order (unlike on_turn_start which fires
only for the entity whose turn just began). For each fire the
formula inspects the team of the entity it's being applied to
(entity[self].team) versus the team of whoever's turn it is right
then (entity[this].team). When the teams differ the entity takes 5
damage; when they match the formula evaluates to the entity's
current hp (a no-op write).

Turn order is sorted by initiative descending: [Goblin (12), Archer
(11), Knight (10)]. Match.active_index starts at the last-spawned
entity, so the first turn-next opens round 1 with this=knight. The
on_round_start hook fires once per entity:
  - Goblin (horde) != knight (alliance) → Goblin loses 5
  - Archer & Knight (alliance) == knight (alliance) → no damage
After turn-next #1: Goblin 25/30, Archer 30/30, Knight 30/30.

The next three turn-nexts advance through the turn cycle. Turn-next
#2 wraps the active_index past the end, firing on_round_end /
on_round_start for round 2 — and on this round this=goblin (the
new round-opener). The aura fires again per entity:
  - Knight & Archer (alliance) != goblin (horde) → each loses 5
  - Goblin (horde) == goblin → no damage
Turn-nexts #3 and #4 stay inside round 2 (no wrap, no
on_round_start), so the totals don't move further.
After turn-next #4: Goblin 25/30, Archer 25/30, Knight 25/30 —
exactly the symmetric cross-team damage you'd expect over the
course of two rounds.

This wouldn't have been expressible before — team was a Python-only
dataclass field, invisible to the formula engine. Putting it in
vars makes it a first-class part of the formula vocabulary, which
unlocks the "friendly fire on/off" pattern as a one-line passive.
The single quotes around the formula are shell quoting (the inner
double quotes around team names need to survive shlex tokenization
to reach the formula parser intact).


SCENARIO 178 — Reading entity x,y from formulas (writes are rejected)
----------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mxy1 XYReadOnly 8 8
!match use mxy1
!ent add a A 10 1 1 10
!ent add b B 10 5 1 11
!eval entity[a].x
!eval entity[a].y
!eval entity[a].x + entity[a].y
!eval entity[a].x == entity[b].x
!eval entity[a].y < entity[b].y
!ent set_var a anchor_x 1
!ent set_var a anchor_y 1
!eval entity[a].x - entity[a].anchor_x
!eval entity[a].x = 3
!eval entity[a].y = 7
!ent info a

Expected: Formulas can read entity[X].x and entity[X].y as
first-class values — they return the entity's actual grid
position (not a vars key; x and y still live on the Entity
dataclass, the engine just special-cases the read path so the
usual vars lookup doesn't raise "Variable 'x' is not defined").
Arithmetic, comparison, and combining with regular vars all work
as expected — entity[a].x - entity[a].anchor_x returns 0 here
because A is still at its spawn position.

Writes to entity[X].x / entity[X].y are explicitly REJECTED with
FormulaError pointing the user at `!ent tp`. The rationale: a
formula assignment like "entity[a].x = 5" could only validate one
axis at a time, which gives wrong answers for legal 2D moves past
other entities. With A at (1,1) and B at (5,1), a naive per-axis
"x = 5; y = 5" would try to move A through (5,1) first — that
cell is occupied by B — and reject what should have been a legal
move to (5,5). Rather than ship that footgun, formulas stay
read-only on position and the user is funneled to the !ent tp
command which validates the full destination in one shot. The
last !ent info confirms A is still at (1,1) — the rejected
writes didn't change anything because the engine never started
the move.


SCENARIO 179 — Special-tile data storage and map glyphs
--------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtile1 TileBasics 8 8
!match use mtile1
!tile list
!tile set 3 3 flame.burn_damage 5
!tile set 3 3 glyph *
!tile set 5 5 loot.gold 100
!tile set 5 5 loot.gems 3
!tile set 5 5 glyph $
!tile info 3 3
!tile info 5 5
!tile list
!map
!ent add hero Hero 30 5 5 10
!map
!tile del 5 5 loot.gems
!tile info 5 5
!tile del 5 5 loot.gold
!tile info 5 5
!tile del 5 5 glyph
!tile info 5 5
!tile list

Expected: Tiles start out empty (`!tile list` says so). `!tile set`
writes dotted-path values into a sparse `(x, y) → data dict` store
on the match; `flame.burn_damage` and `loot.gems` create intermediate
dicts on the way down. `!tile info` shows the full nested data as
pretty-printed JSON; `!tile list` shows the top-level feature names
per coordinate.

The map render picks up the tile's `glyph` key when it's exactly one
character: (3,3) shows `*` for the flame and (5,5) shows `$` for the
loot. Spawning Hero at (5,5) hides the `$` because the entity takes
precedence in rendering — `!map` shows the entity arrow instead.

`!tile del` removes one path at a time. After dropping `loot.gems`
the loot feature still has gold; after dropping `loot.gold` the
empty `loot` dict is auto-pruned (so !tile info shows just the
glyph); after dropping `glyph` the whole tile (5,5) entry is gone
from the sparse store. The final `!tile list` only shows (3,3)
because (5,5) has been fully cleared.


SCENARIO 180 — Formula access to tile data via tile_get / tile_has
-------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtile2 TileFormula 8 8
!match use mtile2
!ent add hero Hero 30 1 1 10
!tile set 3 3 flame.burn_damage 5
!tile set 3 3 flame.spreads 1
!tile set 5 5 spikes.damage 8
!eval 'tile_get(3, 3, "flame.burn_damage")'
!eval 'tile_get(3, 3, "flame.spreads")'
!eval 'tile_has(3, 3, "flame.burn_damage")'
!eval 'tile_has(3, 3, "spikes.damage")'
!eval 'tile_has(99, 99, "flame.burn_damage")'
!eval 'tile_get(3, 3, "spikes.damage")'
!ent tp hero 3 3
!eval 'entity[hero].hp - tile_get(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, "flame.burn_damage")'
!passive add hero burn on_turn_start "entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - tile_get(entity[self].x, entity[self].y, \"flame.burn_damage\") if tile_has(entity[self].x, entity[self].y, \"flame.burn_damage\") else entity[self].hp"
!turn next
!ent info hero
!ent tp hero 1 1
!turn next
!ent info hero
!ent tp hero 5 5
!turn next
!ent info hero

Expected: tile_get returns the value at a dotted path on a tile;
missing paths raise ❌ FormulaError ("tile (3,3) has no value at
'spikes.damage'") so the GM gets a precise error rather than the
formula silently returning None. tile_has is the boolean guard —
returns True/False, never raises, and returns False for both
"path missing" and "coordinate out of grid" so a formula can
defensively pass entity-derived coords without first bounds-checking
them.

The chained read `tile_get(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y,
"flame.burn_damage")` is the user-facing pattern: "what's the burn
damage on whatever tile this entity is standing on?". With Hero
teleported to (3,3), the eval returns 30 - 5 = 25 (hero.hp minus
the tile's burn_damage).

The `burn` passive bakes that into an on_turn_start hook with
tile_has as a guard so the passive is a no-op when the entity
isn't on a fire tile. Across three turn-nexts the hero's HP path
is:
  - on (3,3): turn fires, has flame → -5, hp = 25
  - on (1,1): turn fires, no tile → no damage, hp = 25
  - on (5,5): turn fires, has spikes but NOT flame.burn_damage →
              guard is False, no damage, hp = 25
Even though (5,5) has spikes.damage, the passive only fires when
flame.burn_damage exists — exact path matching, no wildcards. A
more complete fire-or-spikes passive would chain two tile_has
checks via if/elif.

Note the shell quoting: the formula's string literal (the dotted
path argument) needs inner double quotes to survive shlex
tokenization. Wrap the whole !eval argument in outer single
quotes so the inner double quotes reach the formula parser
intact — without the outer single quotes, shlex strips the inner
ones and the formula sees `flame.burn_damage` as an attribute
access on an undefined `flame` identifier, which the validator
rejects.


SCENARIO 181 — Tile data round-trips through !store save/load
--------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtile3 TilePersist 8 8
!match use mtile3
!tile set 2 2 flame.burn_damage 7
!tile set 2 2 glyph %
!tile set 4 4 loot.gold 500
!tile list
!store save /tmp/_tile_store_test.json
!match delete mtile3
!store load /tmp/_tile_store_test.json
!match use mtile3
!tile list
!tile info 2 2
!tile info 4 4
!eval 'tile_get(4, 4, "loot.gold")'

Expected: All tile data survives a save+delete+load roundtrip with
identical structure. The serialized form uses "x,y" string keys
(JSON object keys must be strings) but the in-memory store
reconstructs (x, y) tuples on load. `!tile info` and tile_get on
the reloaded match both return the original values exactly.


SCENARIO 182 — Define a tile template and place instances
----------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtpl1 TplBasics 8 8
!match use mtpl1
!ent add hero Hero 30 1 1 10
!tile def list
!tile def new flame
!tile def data flame burn_damage 5
!tile def data flame glyph F
!tile def hook flame on_enter 'entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - tile_get(tile_x, tile_y, "burn_damage")'
!tile def list
!tile def info flame
!tile place flame 3 1
!tile place flame 4 1
!tile place flame 5 1
!tile info 3 1
!tile list
!ent move hero 5 right
!ent info hero

Expected: The match starts with no templates ("No tile templates
defined."). Defining `flame` creates an empty template that's then
filled with two data fields and one on_enter hook formula. The
formula reads `tile_get(tile_x, tile_y, "burn_damage")` so each
instance's own burn_damage value drives the damage — making the
template generic over per-instance tuning.

Placing flame at (3,1), (4,1), (5,1) writes three independent
tile dicts, each one a deep copy of the template's data plus a
`_template: "flame"` tag. `!tile info 3 1` shows the instance
dict (burn_damage=5, glyph=F, _template=flame); `!tile list` shows
all three placements. Hooks are NOT copied — they're looked up
live from the template at fire time.

`!ent move hero 5 right` walks (1,1) → (6,1), transiting (2,1)
(empty, no hook), (3,1), (4,1), (5,1) (three flames), and stops
at (6,1). The three flame on_enter hooks fire in order; each
deals 5 damage. !ent info: hp = 15. The hook firing message
lists each fire so the GM can verify per-tile triggering.


SCENARIO 183 — Template hook edits propagate; template data edits do NOT
-------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtpl2 TplLive 8 8
!match use mtpl2
!ent add hero Hero 100 1 1 10
!tile def new spike
!tile def data spike damage 5
!tile def hook spike on_enter 'entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - tile_get(tile_x, tile_y, "damage")'
!tile place spike 3 3
!tile place spike 5 5
!ent tp hero 3 3
!ent info hero
!tile def data spike damage 999
!tile info 3 3
!ent tp hero 1 1
!ent tp hero 3 3
!ent info hero
!tile def hook spike on_enter 'entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - 50'
!ent tp hero 1 1
!ent tp hero 5 5
!ent info hero

Expected: Two spikes are placed from the same template. Stepping
on the first deals 5 damage (hp 95). Editing the template's
`damage` to 999 changes the TEMPLATE'S default — but the already-
placed instance at (3,3) still has `damage: 5` (copied at place
time). `!tile info 3 3` shows damage=5 unchanged. Stepping on it
again still deals 5 (hp 90), proving instance data drifted away
from the template.

Then we edit the template's HOOK to deal a flat 50 instead of
reading per-instance data. This change DOES propagate — the next
step onto (5,5) fires the new hook formula (hp 90 → 40), even
though that instance was placed before the hook edit. Hooks are
looked up live; data is snapshotted.


SCENARIO 184 — Placement overrides and per-tile hook override
--------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtpl3 TplOverride 8 8
!match use mtpl3
!ent add hero Hero 100 1 1 10
!tile def new flame
!tile def data flame burn_damage 5
!tile def hook flame on_enter 'entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - tile_get(tile_x, tile_y, "burn_damage")'
!tile place flame 3 3
!tile place flame 4 3 burn_damage=20
!tile info 3 3
!tile info 4 3
!ent tp hero 3 3
!ent info hero
!ent tp hero 4 3
!ent info hero
!tile hook add 4 3 on_enter 'entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - 1'
!ent tp hero 1 1
!ent tp hero 4 3
!ent info hero

Expected: Two flames are placed. The first uses template defaults
(burn_damage=5); the second uses a placement override
(`burn_damage=20`). `!tile info` shows the override on the
instance dict — overrides merge over template defaults at place
time, then the instance is independent. Stepping on (3,3) deals 5
(hp 95); stepping on (4,3) deals 20 (hp 75).

Adding an ad-hoc `!tile hook add 4 3 on_enter ...` puts a per-
tile hook on (4,3) that takes precedence over the template's hook
for THAT tile's on_enter. Stepping on (4,3) again now deals just
1 (hp 74), not 20. Per-tile hooks beat template hooks; per-when
granularity is preserved (the template's on_exit / on_stop would
still apply if defined, since only on_enter was overridden).


SCENARIO 185 — Landmine: template-based self-destruct
------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtpl4 Landmines 8 8
!match use mtpl4
!ent add hero Hero 100 1 1 10
!tile def new landmine
!tile def data landmine glyph *
!tile def hook landmine on_enter 'entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - 25; tile_clear(tile_x, tile_y)'
!tile place landmine 3 3
!tile place landmine 5 5
!tile def list
!ent tp hero 3 3
!ent info hero
!tile info 3 3
!ent tp hero 5 5
!ent info hero
!tile info 5 5
!ent tp hero 1 1
!ent tp hero 3 3
!ent info hero

Expected: Define a `landmine` template once. Place two instances.
Stepping on the first detonates it: -25 damage AND `tile_clear`
wipes the tile entirely (instance data is gone, no `_template`
reference remains — the cell is empty). The template itself is
untouched and still has 1 placed instance left.

Stepping on the second mine detonates that one too — independent
self-destruct (hp 100 → 75 → 50). After both are detonated,
`!tile def list` would still show the template with 0 placed
instances. Teleporting back onto (3,3) finds an empty tile (no
hook to fire) so hp stays at 50.

This is exactly the user's landmine pattern, except now ONE
template definition covers any number of placements rather than
needing a separate `!tile hook add` per cell.


SCENARIO 186 — Ad-hoc tiles are still supported (unique loot chest)
--------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtpl5 AdHoc 8 8
!match use mtpl5
!ent add hero Hero 30 1 1 10
!ent set_var hero gold 0
!tile set 4 4 contents.gold 50
!tile set 4 4 contents.note 'a small treasure chest'
!tile set 4 4 glyph C
!tile hook add 4 4 on_enter 'entity[self].gold = entity[self].gold + tile_get(tile_x, tile_y, "contents.gold"); tile_clear(tile_x, tile_y)'
!tile info 4 4
!ent tp hero 4 4
!ent info hero
!tile info 4 4

Expected: A one-off treasure chest at (4,4) with no template
behind it — just an ad-hoc instance with custom data and an
ad-hoc on_enter hook. Templates are for REUSABLE tile kinds; a
single unique chest doesn't need one. The hook awards the
entity 50 gold and tile_clears the chest.

Hero teleports onto (4,4): hero.gold goes from 0 to 50, and the
tile is gone. !tile info confirms. This is the "unique
instance" path the user called out — unique tiles are still
instances, not free-floating hooks attached to a coordinate,
and they go through the same fire_tile_hook code path with
identical context bindings.


SCENARIO 187 — Group move fires per-member template hooks
----------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtpl6 GroupTpl 8 8
!match use mtpl6
!ent add soldier Soldier 30 2 5 11
!ent add wizard Wizard 30 3 5 12
!ent group new party
!ent group add party soldier
!ent group add party wizard
!tile def new flame
!tile def data flame burn_damage 3
!tile def hook flame on_enter 'entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - tile_get(tile_x, tile_y, "burn_damage")'
!tile place flame 2 6
!tile place flame 3 6
!ent move group:party 1 down
!ent info soldier
!ent info wizard

Expected: One `flame` template covers two placed instances at
(2,6) and (3,6). The party group steps onto them simultaneously
via group-move. Each member's on_enter fires independently
through the SAME template formula — soldier resolves
`tile_get(2, 6, "burn_damage")` (instance data on the soldier's
target tile) and takes 3 damage; wizard resolves
`tile_get(3, 6, "burn_damage")` (instance data on the wizard's
target tile) and takes 3 damage. tile_x / tile_y bind correctly
per-firing-tile so the formula doesn't need to know which member
triggered it.


SCENARIO 188 — Orphaned instances after template deletion no-op silently
-------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtpl7 Orphans 8 8
!match use mtpl7
!ent add hero Hero 50 1 1 10
!tile def new flame
!tile def data flame burn_damage 5
!tile def hook flame on_enter 'entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - tile_get(tile_x, tile_y, "burn_damage")'
!tile place flame 3 3
!tile def del flame
!tile def del flame confirm
!tile info 3 3
!ent tp hero 3 3
!ent info hero

Expected: `!tile def del flame` without `confirm` warns the GM
that 1 placed instance still exists. With `confirm` it deletes
the template, leaving the placed instance as an "orphan" — the
tile dict still has `_template: "flame"` and its data fields,
but the template definition is gone.

Stepping onto an orphaned tile silently no-ops the hook lookup.
No warning spam (a 4-step move through 4 orphan tiles would
otherwise produce 8+ warning lines; the GM was already warned
once at delete-confirm time so we don't re-warn per-fire). Hero
hp stays at 50. `!tile info 3 3` still shows the dangling
`_template` field so the GM can audit and either redefine the
template or `!tile del 3 3` to clean up.


SCENARIO 189 — Tile templates persist through save/load
--------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtpl8 TplPersist 8 8
!match use mtpl8
!ent add hero Hero 50 1 1 10
!tile def new flame
!tile def data flame burn_damage 7
!tile def hook flame on_enter 'entity[self].hp = entity[self].hp - tile_get(tile_x, tile_y, "burn_damage")'
!tile place flame 4 4
!store save tpl_save
!match new mfresh Fresh 8 8
!match use mfresh
!ent add hero Hero 50 1 1 10
!ent tp hero 4 4
!ent info hero
!store load tpl_save
!match use mtpl8
!tile def list
!tile def info flame
!ent tp hero 4 4
!ent info hero

Expected: Template definitions and placed instances both survive
a !store save / !store load round-trip. After defining `flame`
and placing it at (4,4), we save. In a fresh match (no template,
no tile), tp to (4,4) deals no damage (hp stays 50). Loading the
save brings back the original match WITH templates and placed
tiles intact — hero on the restored match takes 7 damage from
the (4,4) flame via the restored template hook.


SCENARIO 190 — Turn order: immediate rebuild on initiative change (default)
----------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mto1 TurnImm 8 8
!match use mto1
!ent add alice Alice 30 1 1 10
!ent add bob Bob 30 2 2 20
!ent add carol Carol 30 3 3 15
!list
!ent init alice 99
!list

Expected: Default turn order with three entities is bob(20),
carol(15), alice(10) — sorted by initiative descending. The
default `turnorder_change_policy` is `immediate`, so
`!ent init alice 99` (which routes through `write_var` on the
initiative variable) triggers an inline `_rebuild_turn_order()`.
The post-rebuild order is alice(99), bob(20), carol(15) — the
new order is reflected by `!list` immediately, no round wait
required. This is also the path a formula like
`entity[X].initiative = N` takes: any var write to the
configured `turnorder_var` triggers the same policy.


SCENARIO 191 — Turn order: deferred rebuild waits for round end
---------------------------------------------------------------

!system set default turnorder_change_policy deferred
!match new mto2 TurnDef 8 8
!match use mto2
!ent add alice Alice 30 1 1 10
!ent add bob Bob 30 2 2 20
!list
!turn next
!ent init alice 999
!list
!turn next
!list
!turn next
!list
!system set default turnorder_change_policy immediate

Expected: Under `deferred` policy, writing to an entity's
initiative emits a `⏳ turn order change deferred` warning AND
leaves `turn_order` untouched for the rest of the round.
`!list` after the change still shows the OLD order (bob then
alice). The rebuild happens automatically at the next round
wrap — after `on_round_end` fires (which runs against the
just-ended round's order), and before `on_round_start` fires
(which sees the new order). A `🔄 deferred turn-order rebuild
applied at round end.` log line confirms the flush. The
final `!list` shows alice(999), bob(20).

The trailing `!system set ... immediate` restores the global
default so subsequent scenarios run with the original behavior.
(Test scenarios share global GameSystem state — leaving
`deferred` set would change every later scenario's turn-order
semantics.)


SCENARIO 192 — Turn order: lowest_first inverts the sort
---------------------------------------------------------

!system set default turnorder_direction lowest_first
!match new mto3 LowFirst 8 8
!match use mto3
!ent add alice Alice 30 1 1 10
!ent add bob Bob 30 2 2 20
!ent add carol Carol 30 3 3 15
!list
!system set default turnorder_direction highest_first

Expected: With `turnorder_direction = lowest_first`, the
turn order sorts by initiative ASCENDING: alice(10), carol(15),
bob(20). Fits systems where the variable means "time to act"
or "agility cost" — lowest goes first. Switching back to
`highest_first` at the end restores the global default for
subsequent scenarios.


SCENARIO 193 — Turn order: team grouping by highest member initiative
----------------------------------------------------------------------

!system set default turnorder_team_grouping highest_per_team
!match new mto4 TeamHigh 8 8
!match use mto4
!ent add a A 30 1 1 50
!ent set_var a team blue
!ent add b B 30 2 2 45
!ent set_var b team red
!ent add c C 30 3 3 10
!ent set_var c team blue
!ent add d D 30 4 4 40
!ent set_var d team red
!list
!system set default turnorder_team_grouping none

Expected: Team `blue` has members A(50) and C(10) — team slot
= max(50, 10) = 50. Team `red` has B(45) and D(40) — team slot
= 45. Under `highest_per_team` blue acts before red (50 > 45).
Within each team, members sort by individual initiative
(highest first): blue → A then C, red → B then D. Final order:
A, C, B, D. The trailing restore returns the rule to its
default `none` value so later scenarios sort flatly.


SCENARIO 194 — Turn order: team grouping by average member initiative
----------------------------------------------------------------------

!system set default turnorder_team_grouping average_per_team
!match new mto5 TeamAvg 8 8
!match use mto5
!ent add a A 30 1 1 50
!ent set_var a team blue
!ent add b B 30 2 2 45
!ent set_var b team red
!ent add c C 30 3 3 10
!ent set_var c team blue
!ent add d D 30 4 4 40
!ent set_var d team red
!list
!system set default turnorder_team_grouping none

Expected: Same setup as scenario 189 but the team slot is
team's mean initiative. Blue avg = (50+10)/2 = 30; red avg =
(45+40)/2 = 42.5. Red acts first because 42.5 > 30. Within
each team, members still sort by their own initiative
(direction-aware): red → B(45) then D(40); blue → A(50) then
C(10). Final order: B, D, A, C.


SCENARIO 195 — Turn order: teamless placement interleaved / first / last
-------------------------------------------------------------------------

!system set default turnorder_team_grouping highest_per_team
!system set default turnorder_teamless_position interleaved
!match new mto6 Teamless 8 8
!match use mto6
!ent add a A 30 1 1 50
!ent set_var a team blue
!ent add b B 30 2 2 30
!ent set_var b team blue
!ent add x X 30 3 3 40
!ent add y Y 30 4 4 20
!list
!system set default turnorder_teamless_position first
!ent init a 51
!list
!system set default turnorder_teamless_position last
!ent init a 52
!list
!system set default turnorder_team_grouping none
!system set default turnorder_teamless_position interleaved

Expected: Setup is two blue members A and B, plus two teamless
X and Y. Blue team slot under `highest_per_team` = max(A, B) =
50 (then 51, then 52 as A's initiative is bumped to force
rebuilds). Teamless are slotted by their own initiative.

`interleaved` (default): each teamless entity is a "team of
one". Combined sort by slot: blue(50), X(40), Y(20). Blue
members A, B come first as the team cluster, then X, then Y.
Output: A, B, X, Y.

`first`: all teamless go BEFORE all teamed. Output: X, Y, A, B
(teamless block internally sorted by own initiative
descending; teamed block sorted by team slot).

`last`: all teamless go AFTER all teamed. Output: A, B, X, Y.
Happens to match the interleaved layout in this specific
setup because the blue team's slot dominates both teamless
slots — but with different initiatives the two would differ.
(Scenario 192 covers the distinct case.)


SCENARIO 196 — Turn order: teamless 'last' vs 'interleaved' diverge
--------------------------------------------------------------------

!system set default turnorder_team_grouping highest_per_team
!system set default turnorder_teamless_position interleaved
!match new mto7 TeamLast 8 8
!match use mto7
!ent add a A 30 1 1 30
!ent set_var a team blue
!ent add b B 30 2 2 20
!ent set_var b team blue
!ent add x X 30 3 3 50
!ent add y Y 30 4 4 40
!list
!system set default turnorder_teamless_position last
!ent init a 31
!list
!system set default turnorder_team_grouping none
!system set default turnorder_teamless_position interleaved

Expected: Now teamless entities X(50) and Y(40) outrank the
blue team's slot (max(30, 20) = 30, then 31 after the rebuild
trigger). Under `interleaved`: X, Y, A, B. Under `last`:
A, B, X, Y — teamless go after the team cluster regardless of
their individual slot values. This is exactly when the rule
matters — for systems where "organized teams always act
before lone wolves" is the intended fiction.


SCENARIO 197 — Turn order: tiebreaker rules resolve equal initiatives
----------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mto8 Ties 8 8
!match use mto8
!ent add zara Zara 30 1 1 10
!ent add alice Alice 30 2 2 10
!ent add bob Bob 30 3 3 10
!list
!system set default turnorder_tiebreaker insertion_order
!ent init bob 11
!ent init bob 10
!list
!system set default turnorder_tiebreaker name

Expected: Three entities at initiative 10 with the default
`name` tiebreaker sort alphabetically by display name: Alice,
Bob, Zara. Switching to `insertion_order` and bumping then
restoring bob's initiative (any change forces a rebuild) gives
zara, alice, bob — the order they were added via `!ent add`.
The trailing `name` restore returns the default for later
scenarios. (`id` tiebreaker would match alphabetical here
because ids equal names lowercased; `random_stable` uses a
hash of `(match.id, round_number, entity_id)` that's stable
within a round and reshuffles between rounds.)


SCENARIO 198 — Turn order: configurable turnorder_var name
-----------------------------------------------------------

!system set default turnorder_var speed
!match new mto9 SpeedVar 8 8
!match use mto9
!ent add ship_a "Ship A" 30 1 1
!ent set_var ship_a speed 30
!ent add ship_b "Ship B" 30 2 2
!ent set_var ship_b speed 50
!ent add ship_c "Ship C" 30 3 3
!ent set_var ship_c speed 10
!list
!ent set_var ship_a speed 99
!list
!system set default turnorder_var initiative

Expected: With `turnorder_var = speed`, the engine sorts on
`vars.speed` instead of `vars.initiative`. Three ships added
without initiative at `!ent add` time get inserted into the
turn order once their `speed` var is set (each `set_var speed`
triggers `_mark_turn_order_dirty` since the configured
turn-order variable changed). Order is Ship B(50), Ship A(30),
Ship C(10).

Setting Ship A's speed to 99 reorders to Ship A(99), Ship B(50),
Ship C(10) — the same `immediate` policy applies regardless of
what `turnorder_var` is named. Restoring `turnorder_var =
initiative` at the end keeps the global default clean for
subsequent scenarios.


SCENARIO 199 — Turn order: removing the team var triggers a rebuild
--------------------------------------------------------------------

!system set default turnorder_team_grouping highest_per_team
!match new mto10 TeamDrop 8 8
!match use mto10
!ent add a A 30 1 1 30
!ent set_var a team blue
!ent add b B 30 2 2 50
!ent set_var b team red
!ent add c C 30 3 3 10
!ent set_var c team blue
!list
!ent delete_var c team
!list
!system set default turnorder_team_grouping none

Expected: With `highest_per_team` grouping, initial order is
B (red, slot 50), A (blue, slot 30), C (blue, slot 30 — same
team as A so they cluster). Deleting C's team var moves her
out of team blue into the "teamless" bucket. The `remove_var`
path detects that the deleted key is the configured
`team_var` and calls `_mark_turn_order_dirty`, which under
the default `immediate` policy rebuilds right away.

Post-removal, blue's slot drops to 30 (A alone), red is still
50, and C is teamless with slot 10. With the default
`turnorder_teamless_position = interleaved`, C slots in by
her own initiative: B (red 50), A (blue 30), C (10). Notice
the team-bucket recalculation — C is no longer dragging
blue's max down because she's not in blue anymore.

(Removing the configured `turnorder_var` itself isn't tested
here because the engine protects it as a vital variable. The
underlying `remove_var` codepath still flags the turn-order
rebuild — it's just not reachable from `!ent delete_var`.)


SCENARIO 200 — Turn order: structural changes always rebuild immediately
-------------------------------------------------------------------------

!system set default turnorder_change_policy deferred
!match new mto11 Struct 8 8
!match use mto11
!ent add alice Alice 30 1 1 10
!ent add bob Bob 30 2 2 20
!list
!ent add carol Carol 30 3 3 15
!list
!ent del bob
!list
!ent hp alice -999
!list
!system set default turnorder_change_policy immediate

Expected: Even with `deferred` policy active, STRUCTURAL
changes — spawn, removal, death/revive — rebuild
immediately. They have to: a deferred-rebuild dead entity
would otherwise still get turns until the next round wrap.
The deferred policy applies only to VALUE-driven changes
(writes to the configured turn-order or team variable).

Adding Carol mid-game inserts her at the right initiative
slot right away: bob, carol, alice. Removing Bob with
`!ent del bob` drops him from the order immediately: carol,
alice. Dealing lethal damage to Alice (`!ent hp alice -999`)
flips her to "dead" status — `damage_entity` detects the
alive-status change and calls `_rebuild_turn_order()`
directly, dropping her from the turn list. Final order:
[carol] alone.


SCENARIO 201 — distance(): the three modes between literal coordinates
-----------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mdist1 Distance 8 8
!match use mdist1
!eval "distance(1, 1, 4, 4)"
!eval "distance(1, 1, 4, 4, 'manhattan_distance')"
!eval "distance(1, 1, 4, 4, 'euclidean_distance')"
!eval "distance(0, 0, 1, 3, 'square_radius_distance')"
!eval "distance(0, 0, 1, 3, 'manhattan_distance')"
!eval "distance(0, 0, 1, 3, 'euclidean_distance')"
!eval "distance(2, 5, 2, 5)"
!eval "distance(0, 0, 3, 0, 'square_radius')"

Expected: `distance(x1,y1,x2,y2,mode)` returns the geometric distance
between two map cells under the chosen metric. From (1,1) to (4,4):
`square_radius_distance` (the default) gives 3 — diagonal moves
count as one step, so three diagonal steps cover the distance.
`manhattan_distance` gives 6 — diagonals cost 2 there, so it's
3 right + 3 down. `euclidean_distance` returns the float 4.242...
= sqrt(18).

The (0,0)→(1,3) example from the function docstring: square_radius=3
(max of |1| and |3|), manhattan=4 (|1|+|3|), euclidean=3.162...
(sqrt(10)). Same-point distance is 0 in every mode. The short alias
`"square_radius"` (no `_distance` suffix) is accepted alongside the
full names for terseness in nested formulas.

The function ignores entity collisions, walls, and the grid edge —
it's pure geometry between two points. For "shortest path actually
walkable around obstacles", a pathfinding helper would be needed
(not in this engine yet).


SCENARIO 202 — distance(): between two entities via their coordinates
----------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mdist2 EntDist 10 10
!match use mdist2
!ent add hero Hero 30 3 3 10
!ent add goblin Goblin 30 6 5 5
!eval "distance(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y)"
!eval "distance(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y, 'manhattan_distance')"
!eval "distance(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y, 'euclidean_distance')"
!ent set_var hero target_dist "$(distance(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y))"
!ent dump hero

Expected: Hero at (3,3), goblin at (6,5). dx=3, dy=2. Chebyshev
distance = max(3,2) = 3. Manhattan = 5. Euclidean = sqrt(13) ≈
3.606. Reading `entity[hero].x`/`.y` inside the formula gives the
live coordinates — passing them straight into `distance()` is the
canonical way to compute entity-to-entity range checks in passives
(e.g. "did this entity move into spell range?").

The trailing `!ent set_var hero target_dist $(...)` shows the
same call wrapped in `$()` substitution at the command layer.
After the assignment, `!ent dump hero` shows
`vars.target_dist = 3` — the Chebyshev result stored as an int.


SCENARIO 203 — angle(): eight cardinal/diagonal directions from origin
-----------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mang1 Angle 10 10
!match use mang1
!eval "angle(5, 5, 5, 4)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 6, 4)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 6, 5)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 6, 6)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 5, 6)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 4, 6)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 4, 5)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 4, 4)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 5, 5)"

Expected: Compass-bearing convention (the default): 0° points
"up" (toward decreasing y, matching how `!ent move up` works),
angles grow clockwise. Eight cardinal/diagonal target cells
around (5,5) give the eight 45°-multiples in order:

  (5,4) up        → 0°
  (6,4) up-right  → 45°
  (6,5) right     → 90°
  (6,6) down-right→ 135°
  (5,6) down      → 180°
  (4,6) down-left → 225°
  (4,5) left      → 270°
  (4,4) up-left   → 315°

The degenerate same-point case (5,5)→(5,5) returns 0 — geometrically
undefined (no displacement) but treated as "no rotation from the
reference" so formulas that compute angles before checking distance
don't need to guard against it.


SCENARIO 204 — angle(): knobs — signed range, math convention, as_int
----------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mang2 AngleKnobs 10 10
!match use mang2
!eval "angle(5, 5, 4, 4)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 4, 4, signed=True)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 5, 6, signed=True)"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 6, 5, reference='right', direction='ccw')"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 5, 4, reference='right', direction='ccw')"
!eval "angle(5, 5, 4, 5, reference='right', direction='ccw')"
!eval "angle(0, 0, 1, -2, as_int=False)"
!eval "angle(0, 0, 1, -2)"

Expected: The default convention gives up-left as 315°
(compass-style, 0–360 unsigned). With `signed=True`, the same
target reads as -45° — the negative half of [-180, 180]. The
"directly behind" case (down from origin) returns 180 under
signed=True (NOT -180); the function deliberately prefers the
positive 180 representation over the equivalent -180 because
it's the cleaner number to write conditions against.

Switching to math convention (`reference="right"`, `direction="ccw"`)
puts 0° at "right" (positive x) with angles growing
counterclockwise. (5,5)→(6,5) gives 0° (target on the +x axis);
(5,5)→(5,4) is 90° (target on the +y axis in math terms — note
that "up" on the map is +y in math because the engine's y-down
screen coords get flipped by `direction="ccw"`); (5,5)→(4,5) is
180°.

`as_int=False` returns a float — (0,0)→(1,-2) is exactly
arctan(1/2) measured from up clockwise ≈ 26.565°. The default
`as_int=True` rounds that to 27. (The validator allows keyword
arguments to formula functions; ordering between positional and
keyword args follows Python rules.)


SCENARIO 205 — angle(): between two entities, used inside a passive
--------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mang3 EntAngle 10 10
!match use mang3
!ent add hero Hero 30 3 3 10
!ent add goblin Goblin 30 6 5 5
!eval "angle(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y)"
!ent set_var hero bearing "$(angle(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y))"
!ent dump hero
!ent tp goblin 3 1
!ent set_var hero bearing "$(angle(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y))"
!ent dump hero
!ent tp goblin 1 3
!ent set_var hero bearing "$(angle(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y))"
!ent dump hero

Expected: Hero at (3,3), goblin at (6,5). Vector dx=3, dy=2 —
target is down-and-right of hero. Compass bearing from up,
clockwise: atan2(3,-2) in degrees ≈ 123.69° rounded to 124°.
After teleporting goblin to (3,1) (directly above hero):
bearing = 0°. Teleporting to (1,3) (directly left): bearing
= 270°. Each `set_var` stores the new bearing on the hero;
`!ent dump hero` shows the running value.

The same pattern works inside a passive — e.g. a "shoots arrows
forward" passive could compare `entity[self].facing_angle` to
`angle(entity[self].x, entity[self].y, entity[target].x,
entity[target].y)` to gate a hit. Entity collisions, walls,
and other obstructions don't affect the result — angle() is
pure geometry between two points, same as distance().


SCENARIO 206 — direction_to(): eight named directions around a center
----------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mdt1 DirTo 10 10
!match use mdt1
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 5, 4)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 6, 4)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 6, 5)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 6, 6)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 5, 6)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 4, 6)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 4, 5)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 4, 4)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 5, 5)"
!eval "direction_to(2, 2, 8, 5)"

Expected: `direction_to(x1, y1, x2, y2)` returns one of the eight
direction names — "up", "up_right", "right", "down_right", "down",
"down_left", "left", "up_left" — matching how each cell adjacent
to (5,5) lies relative to it. Cells farther than 1 step away snap
to the same eight names by the sign of dx and dy: (2,2)→(8,5) has
dx=6, dy=3 (both positive), so the answer is "down_right". The
degenerate same-point case (5,5)→(5,5) returns the empty string
"" — there's no displacement and thus no direction.


SCENARIO 207 — direction_to(): allow_diagonals=False snaps to cardinals
-----------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mdt2 DirTo4 10 10
!match use mdt2
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 6, 4, False)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 8, 4, False)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 6, 8, False)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 4, 6, False)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 3, 6, False)"
!eval "direction_to(5, 5, 5, 5, False)"

Expected: With `allow_diagonals=False`, the function clumsily rounds
to up/down/left/right by picking the dominant axis. (5,5)→(6,4)
has equal-magnitude dx=1 and dy=-1 — pure diagonal — and ties
prefer vertical (matching the engine's per-step facing rule for
cardinal-only systems), so the answer is "up". (5,5)→(8,4) has
|dx|=3 > |dy|=1, so "right" wins. (5,5)→(6,8) has |dy|=3 > |dx|=1,
so "down". (5,5)→(4,6) is again a 1:1 tie — vertical wins, "down".
(5,5)→(3,6) has |dx|=2 > |dy|=1, so "left". Same-point still
returns "".


SCENARIO 208 — direction_to(): between two entities
----------------------------------------------------

!match new mdt3 DirToEnt 10 10
!match use mdt3
!ent add hero Hero 30 3 3 10
!ent add goblin Goblin 30 7 5 5
!eval "direction_to(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y)"
!ent set_var hero face_target "$(direction_to(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y))"
!ent dump hero
!ent tp goblin 3 1
!eval "direction_to(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y)"
!eval "direction_to(entity[hero].x, entity[hero].y, entity[goblin].x, entity[goblin].y, False)"

Expected: Hero at (3,3), goblin at (7,5) — dx=4, dy=2, both
positive — direction is "down_right". `$()` substitution stores
"down_right" into `vars.face_target`. After teleporting goblin
to (3,1) directly above hero, the 8-way result is "up". With
`allow_diagonals=False` and the goblin still at (3,1), the result
is still "up" (dx=0, dy=-2 — purely vertical, no rounding needed).
The string-valued return integrates cleanly with other formula
ops — e.g. comparing `direction_to(...) == "up_right"` to gate
behavior on enemy position.


SCENARIO 209 — Friendly fire OFF (default): is_attackable mirrors is_hostile
-----------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mff1 FFOff 8 8
!match use mff1
!ent add alice Alice 30 1 1 10
!ent set_var alice team blue
!ent add bob Bob 30 2 2 11
!ent set_var bob team blue
!ent add goblin Goblin 30 3 3 5
!ent set_var goblin team red
!ent add wanderer Wanderer 30 4 4 5
!eval "is_same_team('alice', 'bob')"
!eval "is_same_team('alice', 'goblin')"
!eval "is_same_team('alice', 'wanderer')"
!eval "is_hostile('alice', 'bob')"
!eval "is_hostile('alice', 'goblin')"
!eval "is_hostile('alice', 'wanderer')"
!eval "is_part_of_team('alice', 'blue')"
!eval "is_part_of_team('alice', 'red')"
!eval "is_part_of_team('wanderer', 'blue')"
!eval "is_attackable('alice', 'alice')"
!eval "is_attackable('alice', 'bob')"
!eval "is_attackable('alice', 'goblin')"
!eval "is_attackable('alice', 'wanderer')"

Expected: Four entities — Alice and Bob on team "blue", Goblin on
team "red", Wanderer with no team. The default `friendlyfire` rule
is False, so `is_attackable` and `is_hostile` agree on every pair.

`is_same_team` requires both entities to share a non-empty team:
alice/bob (both blue) → True; alice/goblin (different teams) →
False; alice/wanderer (teamless wanderer) → False.

`is_hostile` is the strict NOT-same-team check, unaffected by the
friendlyfire rule. Teamless entities are hostile to everyone (no
allies to spare): alice/wanderer → True.

`is_part_of_team` checks an entity against a literal team name.
A teamless entity matches NO team name — `is_part_of_team(wanderer,
'blue')` is False.

`is_attackable` adds the self-exclusion: an entity can never attack
itself regardless of the rule. With friendlyfire OFF, otherwise it
follows is_hostile.


SCENARIO 210 — Friendly fire ON: is_attackable permits same-team targets
-------------------------------------------------------------------------

!system set default friendlyfire true
!match new mff2 FFOn 8 8
!match use mff2
!ent add alice Alice 30 1 1 10
!ent set_var alice team blue
!ent add bob Bob 30 2 2 11
!ent set_var bob team blue
!ent add goblin Goblin 30 3 3 5
!ent set_var goblin team red
!eval "is_attackable('alice', 'alice')"
!eval "is_attackable('alice', 'bob')"
!eval "is_attackable('alice', 'goblin')"
!eval "is_same_team('alice', 'bob')"
!eval "is_hostile('alice', 'bob')"
!system set default friendlyfire false

Expected: With `friendlyfire=true` set on the game system, the
match created afterward inherits it. `is_attackable('alice','bob')`
now returns True even though alice and bob are on the same team —
the rule says friendly targets are legal. `is_attackable` of an
entity with itself stays False (no self-targeting regardless of
rule).

Critically, `is_same_team` and `is_hostile` are NOT affected by
the rule — they return True/False respectively for alice/bob.
That separation is the whole point: passives that gate on team
identity (e.g. "heal only allies", "damage aura on hostiles
only") keep working when the GM toggles friendly fire; only
attack-legality formulas need to switch from `is_hostile` to
`is_attackable`.

Trailing `!system set default friendlyfire false` restores the
default for subsequent scenarios.


SCENARIO 211 — !tile place_area: bulk-fill a rectangle with one template
-------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mpa1 PlaceArea 10 10
!match use mpa1
!tile def new flame
!tile def data flame burn_damage 5
!tile def data flame glyph &
!tile place_area flame 2 2 4 4
!tile find flame
!tile info 2 2
!tile info 3 3
!tile info 4 4
!tile place_area flame 7 6 5 8 burn_damage=20
!tile find flame
!tile info 5 8

Expected: `!tile place_area` fills the inclusive rectangle between
its two corner arguments. A 3x3 fill from (2,2) to (4,4) writes
nine tiles; `!tile find flame` lists all nine in sorted (x,y) order.
Each placed tile is an independent copy of the template's data —
`!tile info` on any cell shows `burn_damage: 5` and
`_template: "flame"`.

Corners can be given in any order — the second call passes (7,6)
and (5,8), which normalizes to (5,6)..(7,8). The `burn_damage=20`
override applies to every cell in that rectangle. After the second
call, `!tile find` shows 9 (from the first fill) + 9 (from the
second) = 18 placed tiles total, and `!tile info 5 8` confirms the
override took effect.


SCENARIO 212 — !tile place_area: corners off-grid → all-or-nothing failure
---------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mpa2 PlaceAreaOOB 8 8
!match use mpa2
!tile def new flame
!tile def data flame glyph &
!tile place_area flame 5 5 12 12
!tile find flame
!tile place_area flame 2 2 4 4
!tile place_area flame 3 3 9 4
!tile find flame
!tile place_area flame missing 1 1 2 2

Expected: An off-grid corner is rejected BEFORE any placement
happens — the engine validates the rectangle against the grid
bounds, and only proceeds if both corners fit. So
`!tile place_area flame 5 5 12 12` (the second corner is off the
8x8 grid) fails with an error and `!tile find flame` reports zero
placed instances afterward. Following that with a valid call
`!tile place_area flame 2 2 4 4` places 9 cells cleanly.

The third call's second corner (9,4) is off the 8-wide grid (max
x = 8) — it also fails without partial fill, and the grand total
stays at 9. The final call uses a misspelled template name; the
command errors with "template `missing` not found" since the
template lookup happens up front.


SCENARIO 213 — !tile find: empty list, missing template, and template del
--------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mtf1 TileFind 8 8
!match use mtf1
!tile def new flame
!tile find flame
!tile place flame 3 3
!tile place flame 5 5
!tile find flame
!tile def new spike
!tile find spike
!tile place spike 7 1
!tile place spike 7 7
!tile find spike
!tile find flame
!tile find nonexistent_template

Expected: A newly-defined template with no placements gives a
"No placed instances of template `flame`" message — an empty
listing rather than an error, since "zero" is a valid count.
After two placements, `!tile find flame` reports both coords in
sorted order: (3,3), (5,5).

Two templates can coexist independently: defining `spike` and
placing it at (7,1) and (7,7) gives a `!tile find spike` of two
coords, and `!tile find flame` still returns the original two.

Searching for an undefined template name (`!tile find
nonexistent_template`) errors loudly — a silent empty result
would mask the common typo case (e.g. searching for "fire" when
the template is named "flame").


SCENARIO 214 — !undo: shortcut for `!history undo`
---------------------------------------------------

!match new mun1 UndoAlias 6 6
!match use mun1
!ent add hero Hero 30 1 1 10
!turn next
!ent hp hero -10
!ent info hero
!undo command 1 confirm
!ent info hero
!ent hp hero -5
!ent hp hero -5
!undo command 2 confirm
!ent info hero

Expected: `!undo <scope> ...` forwards to `!history undo <scope>
...` with identical behavior — same scopes (command/turn/round/to
round), same confirmation tokens. Damaging hero by 10 brings hp
from 30 to 20; `!undo command 1 confirm` reverts the single
command, restoring hp to 30 and printing "Undid 1 command(s).
Reverted past `!ent hp hero -10`.". Two more `-5` damages bring
hp to 20; `!undo command 2 confirm` rolls back both damage
commands, restoring hp to 30.

The alias exists because `!history undo ...` is the most common
destructive op and typing the longer form four times in quick
succession (during playtest sessions where the GM keeps making
small mistakes) is friction. Note: no `!redo` alias is shipped —
the engine's history system doesn't currently support redo (an
undo erases the now-orphaned future from each retention bucket),
so a redo command would need real implementation work, not just
a forwarder.


SCENARIO 215 — Math helpers: sqrt, floor, ceil, pow, clamp, sign
-----------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mmath1 MathFns 8 8
!match use mmath1
!eval "sqrt(9)"
!eval "sqrt(2)"
!eval "floor(3.7)"
!eval "ceil(3.2)"
!eval "pow(2, 10)"
!eval "pow(2, 0.5)"
!eval "clamp(5, 0, 10)"
!eval "clamp(-3, 0, 10)"
!eval "clamp(99, 0, 10)"
!eval "sign(-4)"
!eval "sign(0)"
!eval "sign(7)"

Expected: Six new pure math functions join the existing
min/max/abs/round/int/float. `sqrt(9)` = 3.0 (float),
`sqrt(2)` ≈ 1.414. `floor(3.7)` = 3 and `ceil(3.2)` = 4, both
returning ints. `pow(2, 10)` = 1024 (int when both args are
ints), `pow(2, 0.5)` ≈ 1.414 (float). `clamp(value, lo, hi)`
pins a value into [lo, hi]: 5 stays 5, -3 becomes 0, 99 becomes
10. `sign(value)` returns -1 / 0 / 1 for negative / zero /
positive. These compose with everything else — e.g.
`clamp(entity[self].hp + heal, 0, entity[self].max_hp)` is a
cleaner spelling of the nested-min/max clamp pattern.


SCENARIO 216 — Math helpers: error cases
------------------------------------------

!match new mmath2 MathErr 8 8
!match use mmath2
!eval "sqrt(-1)"
!eval "clamp(5, 10, 0)"
!eval "pow(-2, 0.5)"
!eval "floor('x')"

Expected: Each math helper validates its arguments and raises a
clear FormulaError rather than letting a bare Python exception
leak as a generic "Runtime error". `sqrt(-1)` rejects the
negative input. `clamp(5, 10, 0)` rejects the inverted range
(lo > hi) instead of silently swapping. `pow(-2, 0.5)` rejects
the complex result (negative base, fractional exponent).
`floor('x')` rejects the non-numeric argument. All four surface
as `❌`-prefixed errors at the command layer.


SCENARIO 217 — Formula functions: define, list, info, call
-----------------------------------------------------------

!match new mfn1 FuncBasics 8 8
!match use mfn1
!ent add hero Hero 30 1 1 10
!ent set_var hero atk 15
!ent set_var hero armor 4
!func list
!func def mitigated raw,armor "max(raw - armor, 0)"
!func def crit base "base * 2"
!func def answer none "42"
!func list
!func info mitigated
!eval "mitigated(15, 4)"
!eval "crit(8)"
!eval "answer()"
!eval "mitigated(entity[hero].atk, entity[hero].armor)"

Expected: `!func def <name> <params> <body>` defines a reusable
formula function. Params is one token — `raw,armor` (comma-
separated), or `none` for a zero-parameter function. The body is
a formula whose trailing expression is the return value.

Before any definitions, `!func list` reports "No formula
functions defined." After three definitions, `!func list` shows
each signature with a body snippet, sorted by name:
`answer()`, `crit(base)`, `mitigated(raw, armor)`. `!func info
mitigated` shows the full body.

Calling them from `!eval`: `mitigated(15, 4)` = 11,
`crit(8)` = 16, `answer()` = 42. Arguments can be any formula
expression including entity reads —
`mitigated(entity[hero].atk, entity[hero].armor)` resolves to
`mitigated(15, 4)` = 11.


SCENARIO 218 — Formula functions: composition and recursion
-------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mfn2 FuncCompose 8 8
!match use mfn2
!func def mitigated raw,armor "max(raw - armor, 0)"
!func def crit base "base * 2"
!func def net atk,armor,mult "crit(mitigated(atk, armor)) * mult"
!eval "net(10, 3, 1)"
!func def fact n "1 if n <= 1 else n * fact(n - 1)"
!eval "fact(5)"
!eval "fact(0)"
!func def loop n "loop(n + 1)"
!eval "loop(0)"

Expected: Functions can call other functions, enabling
composition. `net(atk, armor, mult)` calls both `crit` and
`mitigated`: `net(10, 3, 1)` = `crit(mitigated(10, 3)) * 1` =
`crit(7) * 1` = `14 * 1` = 14.

A function can call ITSELF — `fact` is the classic recursive
factorial using a ternary as the base case. `fact(5)` = 120,
`fact(0)` = 1 (base case `n <= 1` returns 1). Recursion is
bounded by the `formula_function_recursion_limit` rule (default
64); an unbounded recursive function like `loop` hits the limit
and raises a clear FormulaError rather than blowing Python's
stack and crashing.


SCENARIO 219 — Formula functions: side effects and shared context
------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mfn3 FuncSideEffect 8 8
!match use mfn3
!ent add hero Hero 30 1 1 10
!ent set_var hero max_hp 40
!func def heal_current amount "entity[this].hp = min(entity[this].hp + amount, entity[this].max_hp)"
!turn next
!eval "heal_current(5)"
!ent dump hero
!eval "heal_current(20)"
!ent dump hero

Expected: A function body can WRITE entity vars as a side effect,
and the special tokens (`this`/`current`/`self`) inside the body
resolve against the CALLER's context — functions are inline
macros running in the caller's frame, not isolated. After
`!turn next`, hero is the current-turn entity, so `entity[this]`
inside `heal_current` resolves to hero.

`heal_current(5)` raises hero from 30 to 35; `heal_current(20)`
would push to 55 but `min(..., max_hp)` caps it at 40. The body
is a pure assignment (no trailing expression), so the function
returns None — `!eval` echoes `= None` — but the side-effect
write persists, as `!ent dump hero` confirms (hp 35, then 40).

Note: the `entity[X]` accessor takes X as a literal token
(`self`/`this`/`current` or an entity id), not as a variable —
so you can't pass an entity id through a parameter and write
`entity[param]`. Functions that target a specific entity use
the special tokens (as here) or a hardcoded id; SCENARIO 220
shows the `entity[self]` form inside a passive.


SCENARIO 220 — Formula functions: used inside a passive (real self)
--------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mfn4 FuncPassive 8 8
!match use mfn4
!ent add hero Hero 30 1 1 10
!ent set_var hero max_hp 50
!func def regen_amount missing "ceil(missing / 4)"
!passive add hero regen on_turn_start "entity[self].hp = min(entity[self].hp + regen_amount(entity[self].max_hp - entity[self].hp), entity[self].max_hp)"
!ent hp hero -20
!ent dump hero
!turn next
!ent dump hero

Expected: A passive body can call a formula function, and inside
that function `self` correctly resolves to the passive's owner.
`regen_amount(missing)` returns `ceil(missing / 4)`. Hero starts
at 30/50, takes 20 damage to 10/50 (missing = 40). When the
on_turn_start passive fires, it computes
`regen_amount(40)` = `ceil(40/4)` = 10 and heals to 20/50.

This is the headline use case: define reusable math once
(`regen_amount`), reference it from any number of passives, and
edit the formula in one place. The function sees the calling
passive's `self`, so it works for any entity the passive is
attached to.


SCENARIO 221 — Formula functions: validation and reserved names
----------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mfn5 FuncValid 8 8
!match use mfn5
!func def bad x "for i in entity[x].hp: entity[x].hp = i"
!func def usesundef x "x + nope(1)"
!func def max a "a + 1"
!func def dup "a,a" "a"
!func def baditem "1bad" "0"
!eval "undefined_fn(1)"

Expected: Function definitions are validated up front. A body with
an illegal for-loop (iterating `entity[x].hp`, which isn't a
loopable builtin call) is rejected at define time. A body
referencing an undefined function (`nope`) is rejected —
the body validator knows the current function registry plus the
name being defined (for recursion). Defining a function named
`max` is rejected because it collides with a built-in. A
duplicate parameter list (`a,a`) is rejected. An invalid
parameter identifier (`1bad`, starts with a digit) is rejected.
Finally, calling an undefined function from a formula
(`undefined_fn`) errors at evaluation. Every case surfaces as a
clear `❌` error rather than a crash.


SCENARIO 222 — Formula functions: persistence and deletion
-----------------------------------------------------------

!match new mfn6 FuncPersist 8 8
!match use mfn6
!func def triple x "x * 3"
!func def viafn x "triple(x) + 1"
!eval "viafn(10)"
!store save /tmp/scenario222
!func del triple
!eval "viafn(10)"
!func list
!store load /tmp/scenario222
!func list
!eval "viafn(10)"

Expected: Formula functions survive a save/load round-trip.
`triple(x)` = `x * 3` and `viafn(x)` = `triple(x) + 1`;
`viafn(10)` = 31. After saving, deleting `triple` breaks `viafn`
— calling it now errors because `triple` no longer resolves (no
dependency scan is done at delete time; dependents fail at their
next call). `!func list` shows only `viafn` remains. Loading the
saved state restores both functions, and `viafn(10)` = 31 works
again. (The deletion's downstream breakage is intentional and
mirrors how tile-template deletion leaves orphaned instances.)


SCENARIO 223 — Formula functions: built-in protection and overwrite reporting
------------------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mfn7 FuncGuard 8 8
!match use mfn7
!func def max x "x + 1"
!func def clamp x "x"
!func def distance a "a"
!func def is_hostile a "a"
!func def group_add a "a"
!func def tile_get a "a"
!func def mitigated raw,armor "max(raw - armor, 0)"
!func def mitigated raw,armor "max(raw - armor * 2, 0)"
!func def mitigated raw,armor,pierce "max(raw - armor + pierce, 0)"
!eval "mitigated(20, 5, 3)"

Expected: Built-in formula functions are protected from
accidental redefinition. Trying to `!func def` any built-in —
core math (`max`, `clamp`, `distance`), team predicates
(`is_hostile`), group helpers (`group_add`), tile accessors
(`tile_get`), etc. — is rejected with "'X' is a built-in formula
function and cannot be redefined." This catches the common
mistake of shadowing a standard function with a custom one.

Redefining a USER-defined function is allowed (iterating on a
formula is the normal workflow) but is reported loudly so an
accidental overwrite is visible. The second and third
`!func def mitigated` both print a "Redefined ... (overwrote an
existing definition)" message that shows BOTH the previous
signature+body ("was:") and the replacement ("now:"). The
parameter list can change across a redefinition (the third call
adds a `pierce` parameter). The final `mitigated(20, 5, 3)`
confirms the latest definition is the active one:
max(20 - 5 + 3, 0) = 18.


SCENARIO 224 — entities_within: range queries with relation filters
--------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mgeo1 EntWithin 12 12
!match use mgeo1
!ent add hero Hero 30 5 5 10
!ent set_var hero team blue
!ent add ally Ally 30 6 5 9
!ent set_var ally team blue
!ent add gob1 Gob1 20 5 7 5
!ent set_var gob1 team red
!ent add gob2 Gob2 20 8 8 4
!ent set_var gob2 team red
!ent add fargob FarGob 20 12 12 3
!ent set_var fargob team red
!eval "entities_within('hero', 3)"
!eval "entities_within('hero', 3, 'square_radius_distance', 'hostile')"
!eval "entities_within('hero', 3, 'square_radius_distance', 'ally')"
!eval "entities_within('hero', 10)"
!eval "len(entities_within('hero', 3, 'square_radius_distance', 'hostile'))"

Expected: `entities_within(eid, n, mode, relation)` returns the ids
of ALIVE entities (excluding eid itself) within distance n of eid,
sorted by (distance, id). Hero is at (5,5). Within Chebyshev
distance 3: ally (dist 1), gob1 (dist 2), gob2 (dist 3) — fargob
at (12,12) is too far. The `relation` filter narrows by team
relationship: `'hostile'` keeps only gob1/gob2; `'ally'` keeps
only ally (the relation reuses the same team predicates as
is_hostile/is_same_team, so it honors team_var and friendlyfire).
Widening to n=10 pulls in fargob too. `len(...)` counts the
result — the main way to consume the list before formula
for-loops exist (e.g. "if 2+ enemies adjacent, use the AOE").


SCENARIO 225 — nearest_entity: closest matching entity
-------------------------------------------------------

!match new mgeo2 Nearest 12 12
!match use mgeo2
!ent add hero Hero 30 5 5 10
!ent set_var hero team blue
!ent add ally Ally 30 6 5 9
!ent set_var ally team blue
!ent add gob1 Gob1 20 5 7 5
!ent set_var gob1 team red
!ent add gob2 Gob2 20 8 8 4
!ent set_var gob2 team red
!eval "nearest_entity('hero')"
!eval "nearest_entity('hero', 'hostile')"
!eval "nearest_entity('hero', 'ally')"
!ent set_var hero last_target "$(nearest_entity('hero', 'hostile'))"
!ent dump hero

Expected: `nearest_entity(eid, relation, mode)` returns the single
closest matching entity id (ties broken by id for determinism),
or "" if nothing matches. Hero at (5,5): the overall nearest is
ally (dist 1); the nearest hostile is gob1 (dist 2, vs gob2 at
3); the nearest ally is ally. The result is a plain id string, so
it composes with any id-taking function and with `$()`
substitution — `!ent set_var hero last_target $(...)` stores
"gob1" into the var, confirmed by `!ent dump`. (To then read that
target's vars as `entity[<id>]` you'd use the dynamic-accessor
feature; for now the id is usable as an argument to is_hostile,
group_add, distance-via-coords, etc.)


SCENARIO 226 — cells_in_burst: area shapes by distance mode
------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mgeo3 Burst 12 12
!match use mgeo3
!eval "len(cells_in_burst(5, 5, 0))"
!eval "len(cells_in_burst(5, 5, 1))"
!eval "len(cells_in_burst(5, 5, 1, 'manhattan_distance'))"
!eval "len(cells_in_burst(5, 5, 2, 'euclidean_distance'))"
!eval "cells_in_burst(5, 5, 1, 'manhattan_distance')"

Expected: `cells_in_burst(x, y, r, mode)` returns every cell
within distance r of (x,y) INCLUDING the center, as a list of
(x,y) tuples. The shape depends on the metric: with the default
square_radius (Chebyshev), r=1 is the 3x3 block = 9 cells; r=0 is
just the center = 1 cell. Manhattan r=1 is a plus/diamond = 5
cells (center + 4 orthogonal neighbors). Euclidean r=2 is a disc
= 13 cells. The explicit manhattan r=1 listing shows the diamond:
(4,5), (5,4), (5,5), (5,6), (6,5). Coordinates aren't clipped to
the grid — a burst near the edge can include off-grid cells,
which simply match no tile/entity downstream.


SCENARIO 227 — cells_in_line: Bresenham line between two cells
--------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mgeo4 Line 12 12
!match use mgeo4
!eval "cells_in_line(1, 1, 1, 5)"
!eval "cells_in_line(1, 1, 5, 1)"
!eval "cells_in_line(1, 1, 4, 4)"
!eval "cells_in_line(2, 2, 5, 4)"
!eval "len(cells_in_line(1, 1, 1, 5))"

Expected: `cells_in_line(x1, y1, x2, y2)` returns the cells on the
straight line between the two endpoints (inclusive), ordered start
to end, via Bresenham's algorithm. A vertical line (1,1)->(1,5)
is the five cells (1,1)..(1,5); a horizontal line (1,1)->(5,1)
likewise. A perfect diagonal (1,1)->(4,4) steps through
(1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4). A shallow diagonal (2,2)->(5,4)
approximates the line with whole cells. This is the basis for a
future line-of-sight or beam-attack check (walk the cells, stop
at the first blocker).


SCENARIO 228 — cells_in_cone: directional cone area
----------------------------------------------------

!match new mgeo5 Cone 12 12
!match use mgeo5
!eval "cells_in_cone(5, 5, 'right', 2)"
!eval "cells_in_cone(5, 5, 'up', 2)"
!eval "len(cells_in_cone(5, 5, 'right', 2))"
!eval "len(cells_in_cone(5, 5, 'right', 2, 90))"
!eval "cells_in_cone(5, 5, 90, 1)"

Expected: `cells_in_cone(x, y, direction, length, half_angle=45)`
returns cells inside a cone emanating from (x,y), excluding the
origin. Direction is a named direction OR a compass angle
(0=up, clockwise). With the default 45° half-angle (a 90°-wide
cone), a "right" cone of length 2 covers (6,4), (6,5), (6,6),
(7,5) — the cells within euclidean distance 2 whose bearing is
within 45° of due-right. An "up" cone of length 2 is the same
shape rotated. Widening half_angle to 90 (a 180° cone) includes
more cells. Passing a numeric direction (90 = right) instead of
the name gives the same orientation: `cells_in_cone(5, 5, 90, 1)`
covers the length-1 cells in the right hemisphere. Reuses the
engine's angle()/distance() conventions internally.


SCENARIO 375 — cells_in_rect: axis-aligned rectangle area + clip_cells
----------------------------------------------------------------------

!match new mrect Rect 6 6
!match use mrect
!eval "cells_in_rect(2, 2, 4, 3)"
!eval "len(cells_in_rect(2, 2, 4, 4))"
!eval "cells_in_rect(4, 3, 2, 2)"
!eval "len(cells_in_burst(1, 1, 2))"
